interrelatedness, 
grasped in the experience on Monday and determining the response 
in Tuesday’s problem. Or it may not develop until the Tuesday 
problem is confronted. 
Here are the explicit steps in a process dealing with the —5, 0, +3 
situation: 
Step 1. What do these numbers really mean? Taken by themselves 
I do not understand them. 
Step 2. The oscillations appear to be stable and in equilibrium. 
This would require symmetry in the values of the numbers. 
Step 3. The end points are 8 units apart; the symmetrical dis- 
tances from the middle, therefore, are 8 divided by 2, and the values 
of the end points are thus, structurally —4 and +4. 
Step 4. But they are given as —5 and +3. How can this be under- 
stood? Very simply. (At this stage, there occurs a radical separation 
of structural characteristics from external factors.) The position of 
the scale partly determines the numerical value of the end points; 
but the position of the scale, basically entirely external, has nothing 
to do with the relation of the end points of the light ray’s excursion 
to one another, and is arbitrary with reference to the inner structure 
of the event. In order, then, to understand the numbers, we must 
segregate whatever the arbitrary position of the scale may contribute. 
The scale is out of place by one unit, the correction of —5 by +1 
gives the structurally proper —4, and the correction of +3 by +1 
the proper +4. 
Step 5. From the beginning, the place of the zero was puzzling. 
The structural understanding of the numerical values of the end 
points brings with it the clarification of the “0” in —5, 0, +3. It 
appears to be in no way a Structurally outstanding place in the 
oscillation. It is not the presumed location of the light spot if the 
oscillating motion were to come to rest. The “0” is merely an inci- 
dental transitional point, of which the structural value is not 0 but 
+1. The —1 
point, which was not at all outstanding in the —5, 0, +3 
situation becomes focussed as the proper center. 
148 
PRODUCTIVE THINKING 
In this proceeding, assumptions? are implied about the simple 
reasonableness of the structure, such as that there are no hidden 
factors making for one-sidedness, or asymmetry, in the oscillation. 
One of the boys had looked behind the partition to see whether the 
scale was properly placed with reference to the mirror; another boy, 
whom I did not mention before, wanted the apparatus stopped so 
he could see where the light spot would be on the scale, whether at 
0 or at —1! If the “0” in —5, 0, +3 had turned out to be the out- 
standing place, this would have been puzzling indeed, leading to 
the search for an added hidden cause which would make the asym- 
metry understandable. Still another proceeding would be to study— 
if this would be possible with the available apparatus—the velocity 
of the moving light, to determine at what point the positive accelera- 
tion turns into negative, and to see whether this point of maximum 
velocity is at 0 or at —1. 
I have described these events explicitly so that in this elementary 
case one may see that the questions of whole-qualities and require- 
ments are in no way vague, but can be dealt with exactly. For even 
though there are still many who are of the opinion that there is no 
exact way for dealing with the study of thinking from above to 
below, the thinking process in the example described here can be 
expressed in operations with symbols quite as exactly as in the pro- 
cedure from below to above. 
31 did not give the axioms involved in these structural events explicitly here, 
but it is not difficult to formulate them. Besides the inner structural questions, 
there is involved here, as previously stated, a process of separating structural items 
from features external to the structure, much as in transposing melodies. I might 
add at this point that transposition cannot always be performed in a completely 
arbitrary way. In melodies, the general pitch or level of the whole is greatly, but 
not entirely, external to the structural features of the melody; if shifted too far, 
the level may become inappropriate to the structure; the structural features of a 
melody in the bass are different from those of high-pitched melodies. Likewise, if 
one excessively enlarges or reduces the size of a work of art, one may see that it 
© 
is inappropriate, that there is something like a “proper size” (as emphasized by 
the philosopher Georg Simmel). Similar conditions hold in physics or engineering. 
Compare the question of the stability of the 100-times enlarged elephant, or of 
the 100-times enlarged frame building. This is one of the reasons why it is a 
mistake to think that in structures (or gestalten, or “holistic organizations”) only 
the organization in terms of the arrangement plays a role, and that the concrete 
nature of the part-members—or the overall “level’’—is always variable or arbitrary. 
It is, in certain cases, but only when the structural requirements do not penetrate 
to these characteristics.

The relation between the relations —3 to 0 and 0 to +3 is no

call a relation of relations a “symmetry” involves the question of the

is of importance; they operate essentially in opposite directions.

CHAPTER SIX 
The Square of a Binomial; 
Learning Arithmetic 
Tue A-B method has been exemplified in various preceding ex- 
amples—the area of the parallelogram, the bridge, plus three-minus 
three, the vertical angles. The principle of the method is concerned 
with a central gestalt question, the problem of the basic difference 
between an aggregation, a mosaic, a concatenation on the one hand, 
and the conditions of a consistent whole, a good gestalt, on the other. 
I had introduced and used it in very different fields, such as the recog- 
nition of melodies, of figures, and in the study of motor performance. 
It is applicable in nearly all fields of psychology. 
One day I happened to come upon a book by a well-known 
psychologist.t While looking into it, I saw a section (Appendix III, 
pp. 458-464) dealing with the effect of changed data on the funda- 
mental processes of reasoning. Looking at the experimental set-up 
and the results described in this section (before having read the 
other portions of the book), I felt, “Oh, this is fortunate. Here the 
A-B method is already used.” 
Then I started to read the first paragraph of the section more 
carefully. The theoretical formulations seemed strange, especially 
* This chapter was not included in the original edition, although an early table 
of contents found among Max Wertheimer’s papers indicated that he had at one 
time intended to use it in this place. The manuscript for this chapter did not 
secm to be in as finished a form as the chapters that comprise the original edition. 
Some editorial work seemed necessary, but an attempt was made to keep it to 
a minimum.—M. W. 
1E. L. Thorndike, et al., The psychology of algebra (New York: Macmillan, 
1923). 
151 
152 
PRODUCTIVE THINKING 
with regard to the A-B method. “So much the better,” I felt; “if 
a psychologist starting with such a fundamentally different approach 
arrived at the same method.” 
In my further reading, some things happened which may shed 
some light on the theoretical situation. Let us read the first formula- 
tions. The section starts with the following paragraph: 
The older psychology, perpetuated in current educational doctrines and 
practices, regarded reasoning as a force largely independent of associative 
habits, which worked back to correct or oppose them. Our present psy- 
chology finds that the mind is ruled by habit throughout, the correction 
or opposition being of certain more simple, thoughtless and coarse habits, 
by others which are more elaborate, selective and abstract. It defines 
reasoning as the organization and cooperation of habits rather than as a 
special activity above their level; and expects to find ‘reasoning’ and habit 
or association working together in almost every act of thought. 
In reading this, I encountered some difficulties. For example, the 
terms “thoughtless habits” somehow had the appearance of begging 
the question. Also, I felt unclear about such terms as “selective,” 
“organization,” “cooperation.” Are these terms meant, I asked my- 
self, in the same sense in which they are applied to senseless materi- 
als, such as the rote learning of syllables? Or to refer to learning 
in which the teacher calls some stimulus-response connections wrong, 
others right? I found myself bluntly asking the question: “Now, does 
the author mean that thinking is associative habits, or not?” It seemed 
so, and then again it seemed not. Some formulations in the text did 
not seem to imply a basic identification of thinking with such as- 
sociative facts and practices, as for example (p. 462), “. . 
. one’s 
associative habits do not retire while reason attacks.” Or (p. 461), 
“. ., there is a substantial interference with thought (produced by 
an experimental change in the contents of a problem).” Then there 
were formulations such as the following (p. 251) about “abstraction, 
generalization, and reasoning:” “these higher powers in reality are 
the cooperation of many connections or bonds selected and given 
proper weight for some purpose.” These words again threw me back 
into the same difficulty: is this a begging of the question? 
Then, on the first page of the preface, I met the following state- 
ment: this book “treats learning as the formation of . 
. 
. connections 
THE SQUARE OF A BINOMIAL 
153 
or bonds or elementary habits; and finds that thought and reasoning 
—the so-called higher powers—are not forces opposing these habits 
but are these habits organized to work together and selectively.” 
“Good,” I thought; “that is association theory, and the terms 
‘selective,’ ‘organization,’ ‘cooperation’ are certainly intended here 
as they are usually applied to rote materials, to the learning of 
nonsense syllables. But how is it possible to come to such formula- 
tions in connection with, of all things, the A-B method,” I thought, 
“which in my experience seems to show the fundamental ineptness 
of a mere associationist, summative theory of thinking?” 
In their experiments, the authors compare the reactions of students 
to pairs of algebraic tasks. One of the pair is an habitual task; the 
other is similar, but changed into a somewhat unfamiliar problem. 
“, 
.. The concrete situation in the one case is that with which our 
ordinary associative habits have been made, whereas in the other 
case the concrete particulars are somewhat altered.” (pp. 458-459.)

The experimental results showed that the percent of errors is 
considerably greater when the task is of the changed sort than when 
it is of the habitual sort. And so the authors seem to prove the associa- 
tionist thesis, that thinking is a matter of habits. It seems established: 
if the original, familiar task is changed into one less familiar, we get 
poorer results.

gave me a sad feeling. (The change in case 4 requires more—the

cause so much trouble? Of course, the introduction of unfamiliar 
subscripts and of fractions, and not simply the substitution of strange 
terms, such as b for x, c for y, may have been responsible for mistakes. 
Looking at the procedure, it suddenly became clear: this is not 
the A-B method. It is one-sided; it is, in fact, blind to the problem 
of thinking. It does not study thinking, it does not test understand- 
ing, but defines “more complex” in strictly peripheral ways; it is a 
study of half the A-B method, of the counter-influences that come 
from adherence to blind habits, of mechanical learning. It is a proof 
that some processes can be thoughtlessly determined by rote pro- 
cedures. It does not at all touch the problem of real thinking—with 
the possible exception of very routine generalization. 
If I want to test, to study not the role of slavish, blind, rote 
memory and its influence, if I want to get at all to the problems of 
thinking, then I cannot confine myself to such changes, but must 
introduce pairs of changes of the following kind. I shall take the 
first example for illustration. For the sake of simplicity, let us turn 
the problem around, asking for the factors of the expression. 
Oniginal: a?+2ab+b?= ... The learned answer is (a+b)? 
B example: a?+2ab—b? = ? 
A example: a?—2ab+b? = ? 
Externally viewed, in terms of familiarity, in each of the two vari- 
ations one plus sign is changed into a minus sign. But if the subject 
writes (a—b)* in answer to the B example, this shows that he did 
not really think, or perhaps that he had not grasped the problem 
at all. Anyone who is blind to the fundamental difference between 
these two changes just has not grasped the issue. The minus sign 
in the two cases means, structurally, basically different things. The 
minus with the ab, in the A example, means “—ab and —ab”; this 
implies structurally that one of the items, say b, is negative, and the 
other positive. There is nothing wrong with this; —b times —b gives 
+ b?; hence we have the basis for the simple binomial form (a—b)?. 
But what about the B case, where we have the —b?? This means 
that one of the two members must be positive and one negative 
if one is to arrive at a negative product; in the square of the bi- 
nomial, b would have to be at the same time positive and negative, 
which is of course nonsense. 
THE SQUARE OF A BINOMIAL 
155 
The minus sign in the “—b”’ can mean another thing—if the 
minus holds for the entire expression and not just for the b?, as in 
—(a+b)? or —(a—b)?. But here the symmetry of a and b is re- 
quired; the a has to share the fate of the b in a squared binomial; 
this clearly is not true in our B example. 
One may object that the algebraic technique has precisely the 
merit of releasing the mind from such real structural understanding; 
instead, it permits mechanical rules for proceeding. This is certainly 
true. But in productive processes, such things as “feeling the re- 
quirements for symmetry with regard to the fate of a and b” do play 
a decisive role in leading to a new understanding, or in dismissing 
some blind proposal. And even here, in cases like the one just dis- 
cussed, a “feeling” of the structural difference between the two minus 
signs (those of —ab and —b?) seems basic for sensible proceeding. 
There is here also the possibility of theoretically very interesting 
changes (method of the “elegant B”): 
A B change: a?—2ab—b? 
An A change: —a?—2ab—b? 
The A case, viewed piecemeal, undergoes the same, and still 
further, changes from the original. This clearly shows that it is blind 
to deal with “any” changes, or “the amount of change.” The “same” 
change, viewed piecemeal, can mean fundamentally different changes 
with regard to the structure and reasonableness of the answer. And 
this is the meaning of the A-B method: the “same” amount of change, 
or a smaller amount of change, may produce a B 
case, and a piece- 
meally larger change may, for the thinking subject, produce an A case. 
Decisive is the structural meaning of the changes. The reactions of 
thinking subjects are, on the whole, not really endangered if the B 
cases are, item by item, very similar to the original, while the A cases 
involve changes of nearly all items. 
The A case can be extremely different. If, for example, one has 
really grasped the original situation, and is alive to what is at issue, 
one may be puzzled, and not do anything with the B case a*+2ab 
—b?, though it involves the change of only one sign; but one may 
go ahead quite assuredly with such a task as —m?—2mn—n? or, 
sometimes, even with —m?—6mn—9n?, in which almost all items 
are changed, but the structure is not. 
156 
PRODUCTIVE THINKING 
So far we have been concerned with two changed tasks which 
were compared with one another. But it is also possible to see A 
and B reactions using one changed task. For example, the original 
is learned, and then the following problem is set: —a?—2ab—b?. The 
B reaction is (a—b)? and the A reaction — (a+b)?. Now, a subject, 
thinking out loud, may say, “Oh, I have learned that a?+2ab+b?= 
(a+b). This I remember well.” He continues, “What did this 
imply?” Answering his own question, he goes on: “In the example I 
learned, the answer contains two terms, a and b, which are con- 
nected by a plus sign, which is also the sign of each term in the 
expression. Here, the sign of each term is negative; therefore 
. 
. 
. 
(a—b)?.” This approach clearly involves abstraction—more so than 
if new symbols such as m and n are introduced. It involves the 
facing of rules, but in a structurally blind way. 
The A reaction involves recognizing the same structure as before, 
realizing that the signs are negative in the whole expression; hence 
the result is —(a+b)?, with a separation of the inner structure and 
the fate of the whole. 
The impact of the negative signs is still further increased if a 
problem such as this one is given: — (—a?—2ab—b?). I have seen 
a bright boy, looking at this example, smile after some hesitation 
and then write down, despite all the changes: (4a+b)?. Does this 
pethaps mean that he simply gave the learned response in spite of 
the changes? I doubt it. He may have done it by combining the 
first minus sign with all the others, changing all of them into plus 
signs. While working with the problem, he made spontaneous re- 
marks like: “This looks terribly negative. 
. 
. 
. All terms in the 
parentheses are minus; this makes it —(a+b)?. But the minus sign 
of the entire expression is itself negated by the first minus sign.” 
That is, it became ——(a+b)?, which of course is (a+b)?. 
Some other examples: what are the factors of the following? 
1) a?+2ab+ 
4b? 
2) a?+4ab+4b? 
3) 4a?+ 2ab+ 4b? 
4) 4a?+4ab-+-4b? 
5) 4a? + 8ab + 4b? 
One can proceed in a different way than by changing the signs; 
THE SQUARE OF A BINOMIAL 
157 
one can also introduce changes in the terms. 
A most brute B case 
would be a?+2be+ 
b?, or a?+2be+c?. But returning to the examples 
above, example 1) is a B case, because of violation of the structure: 
“2ab” demands “‘b” as a term, while ‘4b2” demands “2b” as a 
term. Example 2) is an A case; in accordance with the structure, both 
the second and third parts demand “2b” as a term. The reasonable 
procedure involves grasping the different meanings of the coefficient 
“4” in the two terms. In “4ab” it is 2 plus 2, while in “4b?” it is 2?. 
Examples 3) and 4) are similar to 1), with inconsistent demands made 
on the terms, a violation of the structure. Example 5) is an A case, 
the “4” in “4a?” and in “4b?” being 22, the “8” in “8ab” being 4 plus 
4 (really 2 times 2 plus 2 times 2); or, one can view it as a transposition, 
by separating the inner structure as follows: 4(a?+2ab+b?). 
A subject who has grasped the structure and its requirements, 
sometimes after a short time of looking at the problem, often sees 
at a glance that 1), 3), and 4). do not fit, with regard to the inter- 
relatedness between the number in the ab term and the numbers 
in a’, in b?, or both; he sees in one comprehensive view, on the 
other hand, that 5) and 2) do fit. Problem 5) seems somewhat easier 
than problem 2); in 5), the original symmetry is there, while in 2) the 
equality of the two 4s—which is not a structural equality—is disturb- 
ing. 
To return to the basic issue. We saw that what is decisive is not 
the amount of change nor the piecemeal similarity of the new task 
to the originally learned task. The reasonableness of the operations 
has an inner connection with the structure, not with the sum of the 
items, the sum of the bonds, etc. 
But even with this we have not stated the real point. The essential 
feature is not performing an operation in accordance with the iden- 
tity or the similarity of a present structure to some previous “struc- 
ture.” The structure at hand must itself be a good gestalt, a con- 
sistent whole, and must be questioned, viewed, in this way. In the 
original example we have an equation containing two expressions, 
one on each side of the equality sign. It is not the basic identity or 
similarity of the left side of the new task with the left side of the 
old task (or the right side of the new and old tasks) which is, rea- 
sonably, decisive for the answer. What is decisive is the interrelated- 
158 
PRODUCTIVE THINKING 
ness of the answer to the present task. The answer must fit in as part 
of the relation 1 (left) = r (right). The equal sign requires that r and 
1 should, in essentials, be identical 
(with the one difference, 
re- 
quired by the task, that r is to be a simpler expression). And this 
requirement, that the two fit, that they must stand in the relation 
“equal,” is decisive. What the teacher taught is not, for a thinking 
subject, that the specific procedure she gave is correct, that one just 
has to perform and transfer. Understanding involves grasping the 
inner relation, the relation p, seeing how r is derived in a sensible 
way out of J, or the other way around, how I is derived from r; and 
what the items in the formula mean structurally. 
A logician might object at this point, “Why do you speak here 
of this mysterious p-relation? The reasonableness, the correctness of 
the answers is based simply on axioms and theorems. The correct 
operations here are in accordance with the axioms of algebra and 
arithmetic; and if not, they are wrong. The derivations are subject to 
the norms of logical validity. (And the axioms are only some axioms 
that happen to have been chosen for the system.)” 
I refer the reader to the later section of this chapter on the 
educational machine (p. 165f). If we think in terms not of abstract 
mathematics but of real equivalence of the / and r, nature could be 
more complicated, so that these axioms would not apply. Still, the 
ptelation, with its fitting-in, would distinguish those cases which 
are simply reasonable from those which are not. Of course we would, 
in such a case, have to drop the simple consistency, and look for 
deeper-lying ones. And we could even imagine that nature might 
make any reasonable axioms impossible; but fortunately, the sensible, 
reasonable approach fits. This analysis also provides an answer to the 
question of the a priori. There is nothing here which would compel 
nature in cases of “‘true equivalence” to proceed in accordance with 
those axioms. Not a blind and absolute necessity, as is envisaged in 
the a priori approach, is at stake here, but the hope and modest as- 
sumption that using reason has sense in the world we live in. 
For that matter, the recourse to axioms, to very general proposi- 
tions, as underlying reality, is somehow unrealistic, as old philoso- 
phers, like Locke, already demonstrated clearly; it does not provide 
the answer to our question. Grasping what is called here “fitting the 
THE SQUARE OF A BINOMIAL 
159 
requirements of a concrete case” is, for children—and grown-ups— 
very much more natural than the conception of, or the ability to 
face, axioms in their abstract generality. 
An associationist might ask here: If a man uses thinking in such 
examples, if he reacts “sensibly,” is this “thinking” not again a matter 
of habits? Let us take one simple example. If a pupil, in solving an 
equation involving an x, brings the x terms to one side of the equa- 
tion, he is certainly employing a habit, and a very useful one. He is 
doing what he was taught, what he had practiced. 
Of course, it can be something acquired by drill, by blind habit. 
But it is, aside from all this, also a reasonable procedure. Since 
the goal is to find the unknown, x, it is reasonable to have the terms 
containing x—and only these—on one side of the equation. There 
is a p-relation involved here. Of course, it is practical to make this 
operation habitual. In dealing with such situations “automatically,” 
our mind is free to deal with “higher” problems involved in the task. 
Nevertheless, there still remains the difference between blindly using 
a habit, and performing sensible operations. For example, here is an 
equation: 100a?—20ax+x?=a?+20ax+100x?. Find x. Now a sub- 
ject, bravely following what he has learned, brings the x terms to one 
side, and writes 99a?=99x?+40ax; and thus Roca Not 
knowing how to deal with such an equation, he comes to a halt, and 
is helpless. But a subject who does not follow habits blindly, but rec- 
ognizes the structure, does not think of starting by bringing all the x 
terms together on one side, but writes down (10a—x)?=(a+10x)?, 
and proceeds easily to the solution. In the first case, the x terms were 
viewed in a way blind to the structure; this was not so in the second 
case. 
Again an objection could be raised here: “The first procedure is 
not foolish at all, for there is a general formula showing how to 
find x with the equation he formed. It is simply his lack of knowledge 
of the general formulas for solving quadratic equations that causes 
trouble here. And it is certainly better to know this general formula.” 
Now it seems to me that this objection is blind. First, we see that 
a reasonable solution is possible without a knowledge of the “gen- 
eral” formula. Secondly, with the “general” formula, similar questions 
M 
160 
PRODUCTIVE THINKING 
arise all over again—blind dealings as against reasonable dealings, 
procedures which realize the structural requirements. 
In Thorndike’s Psychology of Arithmetic? we find the general 
attitude already presented in this chapter, expressed even more 
strongly. “Reasoning is not a radically different sort of force operat- 
ing against habit, but the organization and cooperation of many 
habits, thinking facts together. Reasoning is not the negation of 
ordinary bonds, but the action of many of them, especially of bonds 
with subtle elements of the situation. Some outside power does not 
enter to select and criticize; the pupil’s own total repertory of bonds 
relevant to the problem is what selects and rejects.” (pp. 193-194). 
And “successful responses to novel data, associations by similarity 
and purposive behavior are in only apparent opposition to the funda- 
mental laws of associative learning. Really they are beautiful examples 
of-it.”(p. 191): 
Reading page 192 of this book I was very much struck by the 
description of how children can become confused in doing arith- 
metical tasks. The discussion is about children who are given ex- 
amples of the following kind, after they have mastered adding and 
subtracting with one- and two-place numbers, and multiplication 
with one-place numbers:

Thorndike writes that “they will add the numbers, or subtract 
the lower from the upper number, or multiply 3x2 and 2x3, etc., 
getting 66, 86, and 624 . 
. .” Of course, we all know children who 
would go about the problems in this way. But are they not pitiable 
products of senseless drill? And don’t we know children who would 
refuse to do such blind and senseless things, but would instead say, 
‘dacant do.itae 
. 
Very often, if a child does perform these senseless operations, he 
7 E. L. Thorndike, The psychology of arithmetic (New York: Macmillan, 1922) 
LEARNING ARITHMETIC 
161 
will look insecurely at the teacher, trying to guess from her expression 
what is right, his attitude being, “the teacher should tell me.” This 
is all too usual, when teachers simply issue orders, declaring that 
this is the right answer, that the wrong answer. 
But if the teacher does not, like a deus ex machina, say, “This 
is right, that is wrong,” what about the law of effect? Do psychol- 
ogists realize that the law of effect cannot help, simply because 
it does not really apply? Success can help, but if the child does not 
know whether he has achieved real success or not, how should the 
principle of the law of effect help at all? 
But is it true, as Thorndike and others seem to maintain, that 
the “child of sufficient genius” (p. 192), finding the right proce- 
dure, would do it simply “by the operation of bonds,” by blind 
habit and association? Here is a report I obtained from one child, 
who was not outstanding. “This is certainly very complicated. First I 
will try a less complicated problem. May I? Say, for instance, 14x 3. 
Now, if I have 4x 3, this is equal to, this means, 4, 4, 4. It doesn’t 
really matter here whether I have 6, or 16, or 216, or another number. 
... If I have 3x4=12, this means 
page= 
10 
mes 
; 
twelve, which on the right means 10+ 
2, is the right answer because 
I have the same amount, only put together differently.” (Under- 
standing the “correct answer” means to realize the p, the require- 
ment that the amount on one side must equal the amount on the 
other side.) “Now, 14x 3 means the same thing as 10 
x 3 plus 4x 3, 
and now I have to find out what that is.” And after solving this, he 
proceeded happily to the more complex question, mastering it. 
I wouldn’t necessarily call such a child a genius. The child was 
just led in his operations not by blind habits, or by the strength 
of associations, but by realizing the demand of “being equal,” by 
changing an item without changing its arithmetical amount.

PRODUCTIVE THINKING 
Happily enough, children do, very often, show a very normal 
tendency to proceed reasonably with such questions, a tendency to 
master such things on their own, and not by blind trying. (To be 
sure, there are schools in which these fine tendencies are consider- 
ably shaken in the first school years. Sometimes it seems to me as 
if children were, just before entering school, wiser in this regard 
than after they have become objects of drill.) 
More generally, I did not find children who made the senseless 
mistakes of the first kind described by Thorndike, except in certain 
schools, as the product of blind drill, or when tired or careless. 
Secondly, it seems that there are two types of children who refuse 
to do the problem at all: one type which felt that one should not 
attempt things one has not been taught, the second type not being 
able to solve the problem in spite of trying to, who at the same 
time strongly refused to adapt to the proposed foolish procedures. 
Third, I did find children who (apparently without being geniuses) 
quietly solved the problem. 
When confronted for the first time with such questions as 24x 3, 
one child proceeded like this: “I can’t do this all at once; but, you 
see, this is 4x 3 and 20x 3.”

complicated tasks, like 27x 34, sometimes a child will proceed this 
way:

ae 
7x4 
One faces a different task when one tries to get the child to use the 
short cut, which demands, “you must not do it in the old way; you 
must write down the product (say, of 27 x 3) directly.” Children often 
refuse to do this; they do not understand what it is all about. When 
this happens, I ask them, “Could you do it in such a way that you 
LEARNING ARITHMETIC 
163 
write down only the result?” Some then understand that this is not a 
question of getting at the right result, but of inventing a trick tech- 
nique, a mental gymnastic. And this implies the task of discovering 
a procedure that contains a number of features, such as cutting into 
parts, one of which can be written down, and one of which must be 
kept in mind temporarily, as a different mode of grouping. A realiza- 
tion is required that some numbers can be written down because they 
will not be further influenced by others, and that some must not be 
written down yet because they may still be changed. 
Concretely, this would mean: in the problem 24 x3, I can safely 
write down the 2 of the twelve I get when multiplying 4x 3, but I 
cannot write down the 1 of the twelve, because this other part, the 
product of 20 x 3, may influence it. Therefore I must hold it in mind, 
and add it to the latter value, and write it down only when it is com- 
plete. I have not found a child who could do this without help. The 
reason is not, I think, because the task is too difficult, but because 
the mental gymnastics involved are too strange. (Ability in such 
mental gymnastics is not difficult to develop in many children, but 
individual differences seem to me to be very great. And the task is not 
one of productive thinking, but of acquiring skill in such gymnastics. ) 
“What is required” here is not required by the problem itself, but by 
a certain artificial technique, which has practical advantages. Basically, 
the requirements are directed at a technical goal, not an arithmetical 
one. 
One might think that it is not good to let children use the first 
procedure, which they will not use afterwards; many feel that one 
should not teach the child what he will have to unlearn later. I dis- 
agree. I should think that the good teacher will start with the first 
procedure, despite the fact that the child will not use this particular 
technique. To learn the short-cut method without understanding how 
it comes about may arm the child with routine skills, but it omits 
the development of thinking (and if the trick of the procedure is 
forgotten, the pupil is lost; not so with the other method). 
I think that giving 32x23 as the first task is psychologically in 
error. It not only confuses the pupil by demanding at once the two- 
sided discovery, but also, the equality of the numbers (in the multi- 
plier and the multiplicand) and their lack of equality as to place-value 
164 
PRODUCTIVE THINKING 
is disturbing (2x 3 is 6 on the one hand, and 60 on the other). The 
so-called gestalt law of similarity, which says that one tends to group 
equals, works in a direction opposite to what is to be done. One can 
observe in such cases how the equality of the numbers is an outspoken 
distraction, and a cause of increased difficulty. 
In case the first task, 24x 3, appears too difficult, there is still the 
possibility of helping with 423 or 123, cases which require no 
carrying, and involve no overlapping in the tens place. In any case, 
the best procedure seems to me to be not to teach the pupil the short- 
cut method without real understanding on his part, but to let him 
do the task, to let him find the necessary steps, but in a reasonable 
way, by proceeding from structurally easy tasks to structurally more 
difficult ones—which does not mean that the tasks given also need 
to be easy in other respects. 
The process of thinking in such cases of course makes use of learned 
things. But the procedure is governed not by applying blindly what has 
been learned, as in the foolish cases on p. 192 of Thorndike’s book.* 
The ideal seems to me to be to have individual teaching, fitting the 
procedure to the individual. And it looks as though this might produce 
an astounding saving of time. Of course, there are, even in arithmetic, 
things which must be learned by memorization, but these are very 
few and usually can also be learned sensibly; the fact that there are 
such things, should not color, or endanger, or destroy the other more 
important things, to which these others should simply be an aid. Of 
course the practical impossibility of teaching all things individually, 
which also involves the impossibility of finding enough good teachers, 
demands a kind of compromise. But must this compromise be just in 
the direction of mechanizing minds, destroying abilities? 
Let us return now to the basic difference between the two ways of — 
teaching arithmetic. There is a further way in which one can dif- 
* It is an experimental question to compare, in exact investigation, what happens 
when one is taught a method blindly through drill, as against what happens if 
one acquires it through a reasonable proceeding. For certain purposes, to be sure, 
when one needs not a man but a robot, the first procedure may have the advantage 
of speed. This issue seems parallel to the historic case of teaching medicine as 
rote technique, as against trying to educate physicians on the basis of physiology. 
(See also G. Katona, Organizing and memorizing (New York: Columbia Uni- 
versity Press, 1940)—M.W.) 
LEARNING ARITHMETIC 
165 
ferentiate them. Suppose the children were not taught the objective 
meanings of numbers, but were instead taught the same associations, 
without giving them experience with the real items, without the 
realization of “fitting,” of structures, with regard to these real items. 
In some schools, based on associationism and drill, the teaching often 
comes near to such a state. Would this give the same results, the same 
possibilities? We could set it up in such a way that it would give 
exactly the same opportunities for building up all the associations, 
but would exclude the possibility of real thinking. 
We can “simplify” the situation, in a manner very similar to that 
used in many customary experiments in learning. We will construct 
an “educational machine,” which has a slot on top, into which you 
can put little boxes; there is another slot at the bottom, out of which 
other little boxes drop when you put something in at the top. The 
little boxes have letters marked on them. Now you start teaching 
subjects that when they put a little box marked o f o into the slot 
machine, another little box marked t drops out at the bottom. If you 
insert a box marked t p o, out comes another marked th. If you put 
in a box marked th p o, you will get one marked f. 
Suppose the subject learns this thoroughly, so that in each case, 
before putting the box in the slot machine, he can tell you what will 
come out at the bottom. 
é 
Now we ask him what will you get if you insert a box marked 
t p t? We might get some wild guesses, or a refusal, or the reaction, 
“Will you allow me to try it first, so I can see what I will get?” But 
in all probability we will not get the answer: f. In fact, there is no 
possibility for a reasonable judgment or prediction of this kind. 
Now, suppose that instead of the empty lettered boxes, we have 
boxes made up in such a way that in the box marked o there is one 
little marble, in the box marked t there are two marbles, in the box 
marked th three marbles, and in the box marked f four marbles. And 
p means, put the contents of the little boxes together into another 
box; the answer is what you get when you turn over the box, or what 
you see in the box when it opens and you can see all the marbles. All 
is changed; you will easily get the prediction, f. 
In short, if you deal not with items and blind connections between 
items, but with the contents and the results of the operations, the 
166 
PRODUCTIVE THINKING 
results are in inner relation with the contents and operations. Or, if 
the child learns arithmetic not by drill, but by understanding the 
reasonable inner relations between operations and results, he need not 
be blind. 
Are there mysterious, magic powers involved here? Or innate a 
priori propositions? No. Experience may teach us in these matters— 
and teach us very concretely—that the results of actions are reasonably 
related with the actions done, and with the contents used. 
To put it extremely: nature—or our machine—certainly could be 
of such a character that events within it are governed by other rules, 
such as for example the rule that if you add anything to some item, 
the result will always increase by 1 (a+x=a+1). And then the sum 
of two plus two would be 3 (by logical derivation out of the general 
tule, it would have to be 3), and the prediction, for t p t, of f would 
be factually wrong. A magical world could show such results, and not 
by sheer accident, but by law, by “necessity.” In a magical world, the 
addition of two concrete items to one another could always give two 
as the result, the rule being : a+b=2. For a machine or a magical 
world, this is a possible rule. But one can see at once that there is a 
discrepancy between the equal sign, or the real equivalence on the one 
hand, and the inner relatedness between left and right sides of the 
equation on the other; the equal sign no longer means what it gen- 
erally means, that the whole equation is broken in half, with the two 
halves sensibly equivalent to one another, one on the right and one 
on the left. 
Fortunately experience with the real world we live in teaches us the 
kind of inner relatedness which is outstandingly reasonable because 
of the p-relation, the fitting together of requirement and result. 
If we compare the first situation (the senseless letters) with the 
second (knowing the meaning of the letters), we have to state that 
there are schools in which the learning is similar to the first procedure, 
~ 
or in which children are forced to use the first rather than the second. 
No doubt there is some advantage in getting some operations down 
mechanically in order to free the person to deal with more difficult 
problems. But though it may be necessary for such a purpose, the pro- 
cedure is at the same time dangerous. Because instead of opening the 
mind, instead of giving experience with reasonable dealings with situ- 
LEARNING ARITHMETIC 
167 
ations, it mechanizes the mind and uses procedures that change the 
free and sensible manner of children. 
The procedure would be even more dangerous if most children did 
not, fortunately, maintain an inner resistance to such influences. It is 
true that there are children who react as products of such an education 
when in the school atmosphere, but fortunately, even many of these 
are elastic enough to shed this mechanical attitude outside of school. 
To repeat: we must differentiate, and very strongly so, between 
blind associations, blind habits, blind experiences, on the one hand, 
and the actuality of thinking, of grasping the inner relation between 
operations and their reasonable results. 
: 
The possibility of understanding p-relations in mathematics 
is 
probably the reason why, since long ago, educators have emphasized 
the value of mathematics in education—not because of its practical 
usefulness for certain jobs in life, for buying and selling, etc., but be- 
cause in mathematics we have a material in which it is possible to 
work in a marvellously pure way, by getting directly at the inner con- 
sistency of materials and operations. The assumption of these old 
educators was that in dealing with such materials, in experiencing 
such matters himself, in developing the abilities to deal with them, 
abilities and attitudes are developed which will enable the pupil in 
other situations to look for, to be led by, to grasp the reasonableness 
and inner consistency of situations. 
Certainly the psychologists who base everything on associations, 
on S-R bonds or connections, are in reality not blind to the virtues 
of the second procedure, since they themselves deal with it very 
intelligently. But it looks as if they forget it in principle when they 
want to be “scientific,” or as if they hide it by using such terms as 
“appropriate,” “satisfactory,” etc. They clearly have recognized it in 
the classification they use for these second cases, terms such as “organ- 
ization” and “subtle.” And perhaps they will say that basically there 
is only a difference in emphasis. But to place the primary emphasis 
on the first, on drill, and to make it primary in school, may simply 
put the educator and his methods in opposition to the genuine direc- 
tion of children, who seem naturally to be guided by reasonable con- 
siderations. One may then develop children who will react like mech- 
anized slaves, not only in arithmetic, but in life problems too, follow- 
168 
PRODUCTIVE THINKING 
ing prestige blindly, following fashions, norms, political or musical 
opinions, looking—instead of to the merit of the situation itself—to 
what the “teacher” said, or to fashion or authority. 
There seem to be three fundamental formulations. Either the 
senseless approach is basic, and the sensible is a kind of complication 
of the first; or, there is a basic difference between the senseless and 
the sensible, and the laws governing each are completely distinct; or 
—and I confess that I believe this to be the truest—sensible proceed- 
ings are basic, and senseless ones are just a special case, where the 
inner content, the inner consistency, approaches zero. Perhaps there is 
no natural tendency to act mechanically at all. Maybe acting me- 
chanically is due to a kind of clutching at the last straw of inner con- 
sistency, namely constancy. That men are very often governed by 
habit would then not mean that habit is the basic source and character 
of their activities, but that it is a last resource when there is a lack 
of reasonable possibilities. 
CHAPTER SEVEN 
Two Boys Play Badminton; 
A Girl Describes Her Office 
IN THE previous chapters one issue played a particularly important 
tole: the factor of reasonable reorganization, reorientation, which 
enables the subject to view the given situation in a new? and more 
penetrating perspective. It is this factor that leads to or constitutes 
a discovery in a deeper sense. In such cases a discovery does not merely

PRODUCTIVE THINKING 
mean that a result is reached which was not known before, that a 
question is somehow answered, but rather that a situation is grasped in 
a new and deeper fashion—whereupon the field broadens and larger 
possibilities come into sight. These changes of the situation as a whole 
imply changes in the structural meaning of part items, changes in 
their place, role and function, which often lead to important con- 
sequences.* 
Before the thought process takes place, or in its early stages, one 
often has a certain whole-view of the situation, and so of its parts, 
which is somehow unsuited to the problem, superficial, or one-sided.® 
Such a first inadequate view often prevents a solution, a proper dealing 
with the task. If one sticks to this view, one will often be unable to 
solve the actual problem. On the, other hand, when the change has 
occurred, and the problem has thereby been solved, one is sometimes 
astonished to see how blind one has been, how superficially one had 
viewed the situation. 
The change in structural view according to the requirements of the 
problem is often of profound importance in the development of 
science. This holds likewise for human life in general, particularly in 
social phenomena. 
Of course, such a change of structure is to the point only if the 
proper view has not been there from the start. Often the first view 
has only lacked adequate penetration, sufficient clarity; or some 
demand implied in the situation may not have been fully realized. 
In such instances the solution requires mainly that the situation be 
further clarified or crystallized, and that aspects or factors be realized 
which were only vaguely present in the first view. 
In order to study such transitions and their consequences for the 
role and function of parts, I have sometimes employed specific experi- 
mental sets which bring about a sudden radical transformation from 
a first to a second view. Some simple examples have been mentioned 
“For instance, in Chap. Four, p. 134, “+1” becomes the zero of the “real 
series,” the “0” becomes “—1,” etc.; also in the same chapter, p. 110, the 7, 
conceived first as 6+1, changes into 8—1. 
° Cf. the examples in M. Wertheimer, “Uber Schlussprozesse im produktiven 
Denken,” Drei Abhandlungen zur Gestalttheorie (Erlangen, 1925) pp. 164-184; 
W. D. Ellis, A source book of gestalt psychology (New York: Harcourt, Brace, 
1939) selection 23. 
TWO BOYS PLAY BADMINTON 
171 
(cf. Chap. One, pp. 47-49 ff.).° Often powerful, dramatic occurrences 
take place when a subject experiences the change. The same sets also 
permit a study of what happens to the various regions of the structure 
when it changes: organization and grouping of the parts; changes in 
the location of “caesuras”; centering; shifts as to structural relevance; 
the appearance of gaps, of disturbances; changes as to the extent to 
which local conditions may be varied; changed trends in the subject’s 
expectations, in the emergence of new whole-tendencies, and in the 
direction of requirements. 
When such transitions occur in thinking, it is not the ease of 
arbitrary change as such which characterizes intelligent behavior; nor 
is it the ability in a given situation to produce one or another or a 
third structural view at will, as some seem to believe. Rather what 
matters here, and what is characteristic of intelligent processes, is the 
firm transition from a less adequate, a less proper structural view to a 
more sensible one. And in fact experiences seem to show that sensible 
persons, real thinkers who are often quite capable of making sensible 
transformations, also children, show little ability and even less willing- 
ness to engage in senseless changes of given situations. 
Sometimes the transition from an unstructured and-sum to the 
appropriate structure is needed. But even more significant is the transi- 
tion from a one-sided view, a superficial structurization or misstruc- 
turization, from a miscentered, distorted or poor view to the adequate 
and properly centered structure. 
The main cause of unreasonable, blind behavior seems to be that 
a person sticks to an old view from sheer perseveration and habit, 
which make him ignore or even actively deny the more reasonable 
requirements that are clearly indicated in the situation. 
In order to show more clearly how such transitions come about, 
I will now report on some simple examples from everyday life which 
I studied in various experiments. 
8 Cf. also M. Wertheimer, “Zu dem Problem der Unterscheidung von Ein- 
zelinhalt und Teil,” Zeitschrift fiir Psychologie, 1933, Vol. 129, pp. 353-357 
(See Appendix 1—M. W.) and a description of further examples from my 
lectures in M. Scheerer, Die Lehre von der Gestalt (Berlin: Walter de Gruyter, 
1931) pp. 209-210. 
172 
PRODUCTIVE THINKING 
Two boys were playing badminton in the garden. I could hear 
as well as see them from my window, although they did not see me. 
One boy was twelve, the other ten years old. They played several 
sets. The younger was by far poorer; he was being beaten in all the 
games. 
I heard some of their conversation. The loser—let us call him B 
—became more and more unhappy. He had no chance. A often served 
him so cleverly that he could not possibly return the bird. The situa- 
tion grew worse. Finally B threw down his racket, sat on a tree trunk, 
and said, “I won’t play any more.” A tried to persuade him to con- 
tinue. No answer from B. A sat down beside him. Both looked un- 
happy. 
I interrupt the story here to put a question to the reader: “What 
do you suggest? What would you do if you were the older boy? Do 
you have a productive proposal?” 
If in telling the story one stops at this point, some subjects clearly 
do some thinking. They make remarks which show that they see 
a considerable problem, and they venture some suggestion as to 
what the older boy should do. 
Most subjects do not. They are dissatisfied with the interruption 
of the story, wait to have it continued, are puzzled because I do not 
go on, ask how things actually developed, what the boys did, whether 
they were my boys, why I told the story, why I stopped there, whether 
this was an experiment—whether later I would perhaps give a memory 
test, and so forth.” 
Some reminisce, reflect, meditate, “Oh, such situations are very 
familiar to me. You know I am interested in children. This reminds 
me of my uncle’s trouble with his two children.” Or they recall a 
paragraph from a textbook on child psychology. 
Some make nice subsumptions under general topics: “This is a 
case of .. .” and classify the case, then often embark on generalities, 
more or less needlessly, as, for instance, about social adjustment, adap- 
tive behavior, problem children. 
*The extreme case, of course, is when nothing at all happens. Finished? 
Finished. Are you going to tell more stories? What will we do now? 
TWO BOYS PLAY BADMINTON 
173 
Some want to know more of the facts, ask a number of questicns, 
more or less reasonable ones. For instance, “Of course the older 
boy had had more practice?” Or: “Was the younger boy generally 
slower in his reactions?” Even psychoanalytic questions are asked. 
For the most part these subjects do not produce any concrete, 
productive proposals and show some surprise if asked for one directly. 
It is clear that they have not thought of that possibility at all, busy 
as they are with recollections, classifications, or fact-gathering. 
If asked directly, nearly all do suggest something. Often in the 
tone of “What should be done in such a situation is obvious.” In 
most cases the answers are clearly given without any effort of thought, 
as sheer reproductions of things seen or heard before, as applications 
of some known rule of behavior, sometimes remembered from courses 
in educational psychology. Often they are given in a low tone of 
deep conviction, often with a superior air. 
The proposals offered are frequently characteristic of prevailing 
ideas about children, about human beings in general, about morals, 
current social rules, and doctrines to which the subjects adhere. Often 
one can recognize doctrines of various schools of psychology in their 
far-reaching consequences. 
The usual types of suggestion were as follows: 
“Promise the younger boy a piece of chocolate.” 
“Start another kind of game, say chess, in which the younger is equal 
or even better. Or promise to play, alternately with badminton, some 
game in which he is by far the better.” 
“Bring him to reason by plain scolding. He should be manly, not 
a sissy. Can’t he take it on the chin? He has to learn to take it. Use 
superior authority in bringing the younger boy to reason.” 
“Don’t bother with him, he is a sissy. It will be a lesson to him.” 
“Offer a handicap.” 
“Promise the younger boy that the older boy will not make full use 
of his superior power and skill.” 
The reader may later compare these proposals with the boys’ 
own solution, not merely with regard to fitness—some of these 
8 The extreme case was a nice young man who in this case—and in fact always 
—started at once asking question after question, lots of them. You could not stop 
him. Curiosity is not always, not in every sense of the word, a sign of intelligent 
thinking or of reasonable behavior.

PRODUCTIVE THINKING 
proposals are proper, since they depend on actual conditions in the 
real situation—but with regard to the kind of thinking that is in- 
volved.® 
Sometimes, as I have said, proposals are not made in a facile, 
casual way, by easy recall or application, but after serious thinking 
with a sense that the problem touches deep questions. There is earnest 
deliberation, productive questions are asked. Several times the thought 
processes contained steps similar to those which occurred in the boys. 
I shall now continue with the story. In addition I shall try to de- 
scribe what I think was going on in the boys’ minds. 
1. “What’s wrong? Why don’t you go ahead?” said the older boy in 
a sharp, angry voice. “Why do you break up the game? Do you 
think it’s nice, to stop in this silly way?” He wanted to continue. 
The refusal of B made it impossible. He liked to play, he liked to 
win; it was even nice to be able cleverly to trick one’s opponent with 
the service. B is the disturber, he makes it impossible for A to do 
what he so much wants. 
2. But it was not so simple. At the same time A was not quite at 
ease, he did not feel nice about it. After a while, during which 
curious things were going on in his face—I wish you could have seen 
him as he frequently glanced sidewise at B, and away again—he 
said, but now in quite another tone, “I am sorry.” Obviously a radical 
change had taken place—clearly A now felt sorry that the other boy 
was so unhappy. He had realized what was going on in B, how the 
situation looked to the other fellow. 
Perhaps a sad, quiet look of B’s had helped, as B once turned his 
head toward A for a brief moment. A realized—it was not a quick 
process, it took some time—why the younger boy was sad, why with 
no hope of holding his own he felt like a victim. For the first time 
A felt that his way of playing, his shrewd service, looked to B like a 
® Also with regard to the philosophy of life implied, and the underlying psy- 
chological doctrines which often come out in discussion: 
e.g., the naive pain-

now, then I'll be yours’); on the other hand, a moral appeal which often works

mean trick; that B felt he was not being treated fairly, that A’s actions 
were unfriendly to him. And A felt that B was somehow right . 
. 
. 
Now he also saw himself in another light. To serve as he had done, 
without giving B the slightest chance to return, was something more, 
something other than skill. 
3. “Look here,” he said suddenly, “such playing is nonsense.” It 
was now nonsense not only for B, but also nonsense for him, non- 
sense for the game itself. Thus the trouble had become of a deeper 
kind. 
Expressed in adult language it was as if he had thought—he cer- 
tainly did not, he only felt his way through—“It is senseless for two 
to play together in such a way. The game requires some reciprocity. 
Such rank inequality does not suit the game. The game becomes real 
only if both have some hope of achievement. The game changes its 
whole character, becomes a bad thing for the one player, for the 
other, for both, if there is no such reciprocity; without it there is 
no longer really a game—a tyrant merely makes his victim run 
around.” 
4. Then the expression of his face changed. He looked like some- 
one trying with difficulty to grasp something, on whom something 
slowly begins to dawn, and he said: “Such a game is a queer thing. 
I am not really unfriendly to you . 
. .” A vague idea had arisen in 
him of what a grownup would call the “ambivalence” involved in 
the game: on the one hand, it is so nice to have a good time together, 
to be good friends; on the other hand, there is this effort to overcome 
your opponent, to beat him, to make his achievement impossible, 
which in certain circumstances may be felt as, or may actually become, 
plain enmity. 
5. Thereupon a bold, free, and deeply consistent step occurred. He 
mumbled something like “Must it . 
. 
. ?” Clearly he desired to face 
the trouble squarely, to deal with it honestly, directly. I interpret 
this “Must it?” as “Is this factor of enmity necessary when it spoils 
all that is nice in the game?” Here the pragmatic problem arose: 
“How can I change it? Is it not possible to have, not one against the 
other, but—” His face lit up and he said: “I have an idea—let us 
now play this way: let’s see how long we can keep the bird going 
between us and count how many times it goes back and forth without 
N 
176 
PRODUCTIVE THINKING 
falling. What score could we make? Do you think we could make 
it ten or twenty? We'll start with easy serves but then let us make 
them harder and harder . 
. .” 
He spoke happily, like someone who had discovered something. 
It was a new thing to him, likewise to B. 
B agreed happily: “That’s a fine idea. Let’s.” And they began to 
play. The character of the game was entirely changed; they were 
co-operating, acting together, working hard and joyfully. A no longer 
showed the slightest tendency to trick B; to be sure, his moves be- 
came more difficult, but he called out, “A harder one, can you get 
that?” in a sensible, friendly way. 
Several days later I saw them play again. B’s playing was greatly 
improved. It was a real game. As one could see in his later behavior, 
it was quite an experience for A. He had discovered something, gained 
something that went far beyond the solution of a little problem in 
a game of badminton. 
The solution in itself may not amount to much so long as it is 
seen merely from the outside. I do not know whether badminton 
or tennis experts would agree that it was appropriate.!° It does not 
matter. This solution was not just a technical affair for this boy. It 
involved the transition from a superficial attempt to get rid of a 
trouble to facing the fundamental stuctural issue and to dealing 
with it in a productive way.1? 
What were the steps that led to this solution? Of course, when 
one interprets a single case, the factual basis, the basis for conclu- 
sions, is small. Still, let us try to formulate the essential points. 
Initially A saw the whole situation, and in it B, the game, the 
trouble, centered on his ego (Fig. 105). They were determined, by this

interest in winning by some silly mistake of their opponents. Sometimes they 
even co-operate in order to make the game more perfect. 
In general I think there should be more games of the co-operative, creative 
sort instead of merely competitive ones.

strictly senseless behavior. In fact, the opposite would have been called for: to

center, in their meaning, role, place, function both as to thinking and

PRODUCTIVE THINKING 
Logically, A (as A views himself) differs in the three centerings 
(Fig. 108);!2 so do the other items, along with the dynamic require- 
ments, the vectors, the actual situation. The game is now clearly seen 
as a deviation from the “good game.” 
But what in the structure of the game itself lies at the root of the 
trouble? There is a delicate functional equilibrium in the good game: 
on the one hand, having a nice time together, behaving as friends; 
on the other hand, “trying to beat him.” Attitudes deeper than mere 
external rules of fair play make this delicate equilibrium possible, 
establish the differences between a good game and a ruthless fight 
or a ruthless competition; in short, make a game a game. The equilib- 
rium is psychologically delicate; it may disappear—as it did in this 
situation. 
The “against,” the “trying to beat him,” that functions properly in 
the good game had grown into an ugly feature which no longer 
fitted the game situation. That gave rise to the vector: “What could 
be done about it? And done immediately?” Here lies the trouble. 
“Is it not possible to get at the core of the situation?” This leads to 
considering structure II. 
Structure Ia ————> <———— or, with these boys, rather 
Structure Ib ——————> <—— is transformed into 
Structure II, Tinta competing to co-operation; from I against 
You, to We. A and B, as parts, are here no longer as in Structure I, 
opponents, each for his own side only, but two human beings co- 
operating toward a common goal. 
All the items change their meaning considerably. For instance, 
serving no longer means that B is to be overwhelmed by making a 
return of the bird impossible. In the first state of affairs (1), a player 
is happy if he scores and the other player misses; but now (II), each 
enjoys every good rally directly. 
The steps which then follow show the transition to facing the 
problem situation on its merits rather than from the viewpoint of 
one side or the other, or of the mere sum of both. The solution 
Cf. M. Wertheimer, “On truth,” Social Research, 1934, Vol. 1, pp. 135-146. 
TWO BOYS PLAY BADMINTON 
179 
grows out of the realization of the structural trouble; then the trouble 
acquires a deeper meaning. The tension is not overcome superficially; 
rather, the new direction of the vectors follows from basic structural 
requirements which demand a genuinely good situation. You may 
think I read far too much into the boy’s mind. I don’t think so. You 
may have far too poor an idea of what can happen in the minds of 
boys. 
In short we note specifically: 
1) Operations of recentering: transition from a one-sided view 
to the centering required by the objective structure of the situation; 
2) a change of meaning of the parts—and of the vectors—in ac- 
cordance with their structural place, role and function; 
3) a view of the situation in terms of “good structure,” so that 
everything fits the structural requirements; 
4) a trend to go straight to fundamentals, to face the issue hon- 
estly, and to draw the consequences. 
I want to remark that the feature of straightness, honesty, sin- 
cerity, does not seem peripheral in such a process. Generally speaking, 
it is an artificial and narrow view which conceives of thinking as 
only an intellectual operation, and separates it entirely from questions 
of human attitude, feeling, and emotion—“because such topics be- 
long to other chapters of psychology.” This is especially clear in one 
example, in the transition from a blind egocentric view with its 
emotional ingredients to the later steps. But even seemingly mere 
intellectual processes involve a human attitude—that kind of willing- 
ness to face issues, to deal with them frankly, honestly, and sincerely. 
Although I have referred to this fact only briefly in other chapters, it 
seems essential in many cases of productive thinking, including even 
our problems in pure elementary geometry. 
Of course it may happen that certain solutions are found when the 
subject assumes just the opposite attitude, when he tries to subdue the 
situation by a trick or to do violence to it. But here too such factors 
seem not merely to accompany the “intellectual operations”; rather, 
the very nature of the operations, their genesis, and their develop- 
ment seem deeply connected with the actual human attitude toward 
the problem and its solution. Neither slyness, nor a spirit of domineer- 
ing seems to be the most advisable attitude in productive thinking, 
180 
PRODUCTIVE THINKING 
even though it may sometimes lead to practical success, and thus 
make for a certain quick, short-range efficiency. 
Connected with these matters is a further point which seems of 
fundamental importance for a true understanding of productive 
thinking both in the theoretical and in the practical realm. This is 
the transition from a first stage in which the subject simply wants 
to reach a certain goal—in which his thought is entirely centered 
by this goal—to another stage in which the vectors, the operations, 
the actions become centered by more fundamental requirements 
of the situation. In a sense a subject may become virtually blind if 
he looks only at that goal, and is entirely governed by the urge 
toward it. Often he must first forget what he happens to wish before 
he can become susceptible to what the situation itself requires. And 
so, in better instances, his attitude will be far more similar to the 
attitude of a physician or a wise adviser than to that of a clever 
and domineering conqueror or aggressor. 
This transition is one of the great moments in many genuine 
thought processes. The role of the merely subjective interests of the 
self is, I think, much overestimated in human actions. Real thinkers 
forget about themselves in thinking. The main vectors in genuine 
thought often do not refer to the I with its personal interests; rather, 
they represent the structural requirements of the given situation.1% 
Or when such vectors do refer to an I, this is not just the I as center 
of subjective striving. 
Of course the transition may lie also in the direction of the deeper 
requirements of the I itself. Sometimes there is a happy coincidence 
between the requirements of the situation which presents the prob- 
lem and the real, the deeper needs of the I, as was the case with 
our boy. 
This was only a modest little story. Yet some of its characteristics, 
as, for instance, the essential progress which occurred through a 
change of centering, are, I think, characteristic of profoundly im- 
portant accomplishments" in human beings, in human society. One 
can sometimes observe marvelous changes in individuals, as when 
18 See E. Levy, “Some aspects of the schizophrenic formal disturbance of 
thought,” Psychiatry, 1943, Vol. 6, pp. 55-69. 
14M. Wertheimer, “A story of three days.” In R. N. Anshen (Ed.), Freedom: 
its meaning (New York: Harcourt, Brace, 1940) pp. 555-569. 
A GIRL DESCRIBES HER OFFICE 
181 
some passionately biased person becomes a member of a jury, or 
arbitrator, or judge, and when his actions then show the fine transi- 
tion from bias to an honest effort to deal with the problems at issue 
in a just and objective fashion. The very development of the idea 
and institution of courts of justice is at issue here. 
Centering—the way one views the parts, the items in a situation, 
their meaning and role as determined in regard to a center, a core, 
or radix—is a most powerful factor in thinking. The problems of 
centering have been neglected in traditional logic and in psychology. 
Strong forces are at work in centering, in envisaging—or in trying 
to envisage—the true center according to the nature of a situation; 
but they are equally strong in cases of blind, enforced or willful 
miscentering, so efficiently used in some kinds of political propa- 
ganda. Although there are many strong forces working against true 
centering, there is nevertheless in human beings a clear desire not to 
be structurally blind, a desire, to center properly, to do justice to 
the situation, to center in accordance with the nature of the object, 
of the structural objective requirements. 
As to the concept of centering, it seems to be tacitly recognized 
that adequate centering, with its consequences for objectivity and 
justice, is of the utmost importance. Otherwise, why should actual 
miscentering be the more energetically disguised as true and just 
centering, the less it is so in fact? 
Quite a number of things are involved in the process of recentering 
as it occurred in the badminton example. Some basic points will be- 
come clearer if we now consider a simpler story. 
I was visiting a family. The daughter of the house came home and 
was introduced to me. Her father asked how she had spent the day. 
She answered that there had been a good deal of work, but that 
she was fine. I asked, “‘Are you working?” “Yes,” she answered, “I 
am working with a firm.” “Is it a big place?” “Well,” she said, “there 
are a number of people in the office. I have to do directly with a 
Mr. A, a Mr. B, and a Mr. C, who often come to my desk, ask ques- 
tions, bring me letters, etc. There are others in the office with whom 
I do not have to do directly. Mr. A has dealings with a Mr. D, Mr. B 
182 
PRODUCTIVE THINKING 
with a Mr. E, and Mr. C with a Mr. F. D and E also have dealings 
with each other; so do E and F. Let me see, that makes six people in 
the office besides myself.” 
I asked, “Are you the boss?” “Oh no,” she answered. “Do you give 
orders to anyone?” “Oh yes, I sometimes give orders to Mr. A and 
to Mr. C. I get orders from Mr. B; Mr. D gets them from Mr. E, Mr. 
E from Mr. B, and Mr. F from Mr. E.” (She was apparently logically 
minded, and was trying to tell the whole story.) 
I was somewhat disturbed—I guess the reader is too—and I said: 
“T am still in the dark about the people in your office.” “Oh, but I 
have told you everything,” she answered. Nevertheless, I was in the 
dark. Suddenly I said—it was a hunch—“So Mr. B is your boss, and 
you are directly below him, and so is Mr. E?” “Yes,” she said. 
As she saw her office, she had stated the relations correctly and 
completely, and yet she had not made the real picture clear. Most 
people, if asked such a question, would start with some such state- 
ment as “I am working directly under the Boss B; so is a Mr. E”; they 
might add, “Mr. E and I each have two people working under us to 
whom we give orders,” and might continue, “T'wo of these some- 
times have business with each other.” ‘This would have been a sensible 
description; it would have given a clear picture of the structure of the 
office setup. But this girl had enumerated the men and the relations 
in a bewildering succession, in fact in a way that was blind to the 
structure of the situation; she had centered everything about her ego 
—except for the last bewildering statement in the second description 
which did not refer to her. 
This is an innocent example of a foolish attitude in life and in 
thinking, which often has considerable consequences for shaping 
one’s views and one’s actions. 
Of course it might have been mere clumsiness in description, but 
from the girl’s subsequent remarks and behavior it became clear 
that this was characteristic of her real attitudes. Some time later, 
when I met one of her coworkers from the office, I asked him how 
she was getting on. “Quite nicely,” he said, “she is a fine person. 
But we are not certain whether her job will last very long. She has 
a funny way of behaving toward the others and even toward her 
work. She seems to relate all things to herself, as if she were always 
A GIRL DESCRIBES HER OFFICE 
183 
the center of the situation, even in business matters when ncbody 
is thinking of her personally. That is not good for business.” 
When self-centering is extreme, it becomes a well-known symptom 
of a psychopathological state that often leads to a precarious situa- 
tion both in social and in personal affairs.15 Self-centering is not at 
all the general, the natural attitude, as some influential views of 
our time would have us believe. 
Let us look more closely at what this girl did in her description. 
In a schema it may be represented as follows.

Everything is now structurally clear; there is no confusion as in the 
previous descriptions, schemas, and diagrams. The girl had stated 
all the relations involved, but in a rather disturbing, miscentered 
aggregate. The first and the second descriptions and the corre- 
sponding figures go against the objective structure of the situation: 
they are blind to it, they violate it, they center it wrongly. There is 
nothing to be said against starting with the Ego in the description, 
but in a reasonable description one would not center all that follows 
about it. On the contrary, one would see and report the Ego in its 
(secondary) place within the structure. 
If the reader compares the first and the second with the third or 
the fourth diagram, he will see what I mean when I say that in the 
first and the second the succession, the grouping, the centering are 
done in a way that is blind to the structurally adequate picture. 
But let us look more closely: if we wish to characterize the place,

The result is similar if we take into account the directed character 
of the relations in the second and fourth descriptions. 
We find that in this example there are three kinds of people; we 
may call them boss, secretaries, clerks. Centered about the Ego, they 
are considerably mixed up in the groupings of the first and second 
descriptions. In the corresponding schemas we find, as members of 
one group, two clerks and one boss; and as members of another 
group, two clerks and one secretary; one secretary, the girl, is a 
single item on the left side, whereas the other secretary is one mem- 
ber of a triplet on the right side. Similarly with the relations: on 
the left side of the relational schema (pp. 183-184) we have two re- 
lations between secretary and clerks, one between secretary and boss; 
further to the right two relations between clerks, one between boss 
and secretary; to the extreme right two relations between secretary 
and clerks. 
In contrast, everything is in clear, surveyable, flawless order when 
we turn to the third and fourth descriptions and schemas (p. 185). 
The starting point is the boss, then come the two secretaries, and 
finally the four clerks. The first two relations are the relations be- 
tween boss and secretaries, then follow the relations between sec- 
retaries and clerks, and finally the relations between the clerks. 
In short: viewed piecemeal, the first and second descriptions are 
in a way correct, and complete in all details, but they introduce a 
centering, a grouping that is blind to, and in violation of, the logical 
hierarchy in the situation. They put together things which are dif- 
ferent in nature and let things appear as structurally different which 
are not. Seen from the girl’s subjective point of view, i.e., without 
A GIRL DESCRIBES HER OFFICE 
189 
taking her secondary position into account, the description distorts 
the structure; it fails to do justice to the structural meanings of the 
parts. 
What we have just said concerning the difference between mis- 
centered description and structurally appropriate description applies 
even if the situation is characterized in terms of implicit definitions, 
_relation-numbers, and so forth. (Nevertheless, the question of clear- 
ness of classes as expressed in such terms is not the real heart of the 
matter. The reader will be aware of the fact that we must exercise 
caution when we use this logistical method.)
