Fifth: another boy, twelve years old, without training in geometry, 
started boasting about how good he was in such problems, and made 
wild guesses with great assurance. For example: “The four remainders 
are a quarter of the circle.” I said to him, “Don’t talk nonsense. 
Think a bit.” He was silent half a minute, and then said, “If you 
move both upper remainders up, and have them fit the upper semi- 
circle, and if you do the same with the two lower remainders, both 
things taken together make a square! That’s it.” 
APPENDIX 3 
The School Inspector’ 
I rEPEAT what I stressed in Chapter One (and elsewhere): there are 
items or features in a situation which are structurally central, and 
others which are not, which are structurally peripheral, variable. For 
example, the absolute lengths of the auxiliary lines of the parallelo- 
gram are not involved in the structural interrelatedness—not much 
more than the color of the parallelogram. 
To see, to grasp, to realize what is structurally central and what 
is not, is, in most cases of thinking, of the highest importance. One 
very extreme example was mentioned in section 14 of Chapter One, 
in which an hypothesis was put forth—the succession of the numbers 
by one—which has nothing to do with the structure implied in the 
problem. 
In order to illustrate the issue I shall tell a story of an entirely 
different kind. The events are said to have happened in a small 
Moravian village in the time of the old Austrian empire. An inspector 
from the Ministry of Education arrived one day to visit the school 
room. It was part of his duty to make such periodic inspections of 
the schools. At the end of the hour, after he had observed the class, 
he stood up and said: “I am glad to see that you children are doing 
well in your studies. You are a good class; I am satisfied with your 
progress. Therefore, before I go, there is one question I would like 
to ask: How many hairs does a horse have?” Very quickly one little 
nine-year-old boy raised his hand, to the astonishment of the teacher 
and the visitor. The boy stood up, and said: “The horse has 3,571,962 
* This appendix, which was not included in the original edition, is a slightly 
edited version of a fragment found among Max Wertheimer’s papers——M. W. 
269 
270 
PRODUCTIVE THINKING 
hairs.” The inspector wonderingly asked: “And how do you know that 
this is the right number?” The boy replied: “If you do not believe 
me, you could count them yourself.” The inspector broke into loud 
laughter, thoroughly enjoying the boy’s remark. As the teacher escorted 
him along the aisle to the door, still laughing heartily, he said: “What 
an amusing story! I must tell it to my colleagues when I return to 
Vienna. I can already see how they will take it; they enjoy nothing 
better than a good joke.” And with that he took his leave. 
It is a year later, the inspector is back again at the village school 
for his annual visit. As the teacher was walking along the aisle with 
him to the door, he stopped and said: “By the way, Mr. Inspector, 
how did your colleagues like the story of the horse and the number 
of his hairs?” The inspector slapped the teacher on the back. “Oh, 
yes,” he said. “You know, I was really very anxious to tell this story 
—and a fine story it was—but, you see, I couldn’t. When I got back 
to Vienna I wasn’t able for the life of me to remember the number 
of hairs.” 
This is an invented story; at least I hope it is. I asked many people, 
after they had heard the story: “Now what is the point of the story?” 
One type of answer: “It’s really a stupid story; this inspector was 
apparently thinking in a way which violates the old logical differences 
between essentials and unessentials.” I said: “Certainly, but could 
you please tell me what you mean here by essential?” Most people 
have no way of saying this (besides feeling no need to explain this 
obvious thing). Some do; some in clumsy and odd ways; some use the 
historical proposals for the meaning of unessential, like not being 
constant, etc—which begs the question, which is in reality no answer 
because it already presupposes the answer. 
Some say, rightly: “You see, it doesn’t matter what the number 
in the story is.” I asked: “That’s right, but will you please tell me 
why?” And then sometimes the “essential” answer develops: “The 
content of the number has nothing to do with the central issue of 
the story; there is no interdependence; or, better, there is no reason- 
able, inner relation between the whole story and just this number 
(no p-relation). Therefore, the item is reasonably variable.” The 
function of the item in its place and role in the structure has nothing 
to do with being just such and such a number. The structure sets no 
THE SCHOOL INSPECTOR 
271 
functional requirement for the exact amount. What the structure re- 
quires here is fulfilled by any (large) number. 
And why is this story often seen as a rather good joke? Because 
of the surprise at seeing the foolish determination to stick to just 
this one item, as if it were in its specific content a relevant item in 
the structure. It is funny to see how blind to the structure the In- 
spector’s behavior is. I might add that some are a little disturbed, not 
being able to bring together the story and the reaction to it, and that 
some do not seem to think at all in the direction of clarifying what 
was involved in the thought operation of the Inspector, but speak 
in terms of probable personality traits of the Inspector. 
Such personality problems are important, but a different point 
of view is also needed, to conceive clearly what this behavior meant 
structurally. Perhaps the occurrence of such a mental attitude 1s, 
in such cases, not at all a question of individual personality char- 
acteristics, but of a trend created by certain kinds of education (based 
on certain tendencies in theoretical psychology)—and only exag- 
gerated in such a joke. 
APPENDIX 4 
Suggestions for Teaching About Area’ 
Wuicu procedure in teaching a proof is psychologically good and 
which may not be good psychologically—these are questions to be 
decided empirically. It is a question of comparing different ways of 
teaching with regard to difficulties in learning, retention, the pos- 
sibility of reconstruction, and of application to other problems. The 
question concerns not only a mastery of certain issues, but also an 
orientation in the subject matter, an opening of the mind, develop- 
ment of abilities to think. 
These are matters of experience and experimentation. But the 
need is not simply to compare blindly any kinds of teaching, but 
rather to try to study the effects of extremes with regard to our main 
issue: structural reasonability or structural arbitrariness in the teach- 
ing itself. 
Here I shall sketch one of the possible procedures which the 
teacher could employ to teach a problem in a psychologically rea- 
sonable way. 
It seems desirable for many children—not for all—to start with 
concrete, actual life situations in which the task, say finding the area 
of a rectangle, is reasonably required. One can do this, for example, 
with a story of two farmers who exchange two plots of land, or with 
the story of a farmer who is trying to find out how much seed he 
will need for a certain portion of his land—questions which sensibly 
demand the determination of the area. 
* This appendix, which was not included in the original edition, is a slightly 
edited version of a fragment found among Max Wertheimer’s papers —M. W. 
272 
TEACHING ABOUT AREA 
273 
For such children it makes a big difference whether or not there 
is some real sense in putting the problem at all. For illustration I 
may describe the following instances. 
Twenty-five years ago the parents of a nine-year old girl, friends 
of mine, told me that she experienced great difficulties in her school 
work. So much was this the case that the child’s teacher had advised 
them to turn to a psychiatrist for advice. The parents brought the 
child to Professor Z, who was a famous psychiatrist and psychologist, 
at that time head of the psychiatric clinic. They told me sadly that 
he had tested her and had advised that she be taken out of school, 
her intelligence being too low. He proposed that she be placed in a 
home for the feeble-minded. He ended by warning the parents that if 
they failed to do this the child would become maladjusted and possibly 
delinquent. 
The parents could not bring themselves to follow this advice. They 
felt that the verdict of the psychologist could not be true of their 
child, who very often showed excellent sense, especially in human 
situations, and who also had a sensitive feeling for art. But, on the 
other hand, there were the school difficulties, especially in arithmetic. 
That the intelligence test of Professor Z showed such poor results 
was in agreement with her failure in schoolwork. 
They asked for my opinion. Knowing the child from infancy, know- 
ing her as a fine child, I proposed to speak to her. I first asked her to 
tell me what questions Professor Z had put to her. There were the 
usual problems, including among others arithmetical tasks which 
required adding, subtracting, multiplying, dividing, etc. She was 
unable to solve these problems. I changed the topic of discussion. 
Then I told her of a concrete situation which, according to my story, 
developed in a neighboring family, in which the mother was preparing 
a bath for the baby, without having the right containers. The child 
was keenly interested in the story; and when, at the dramatic moment, 
I put the question, “What to do?” she reacted vividly and success- 
fully. And this despite the fact that I had chosen problems demand- 
ing not only operations Professor Z had tested, but also others more 
difficult. This child was not able to deal with problems the sense of 
which she did not see. On the other hand, if a problem grew out of 
the situation, if the solution was required by the situation, she en- 
274 
PRODUCTIVE THINKING 
countered no unusual difficulty, frequently showing excellent sense. 
A friend of mine, an anthropologist at Liibeck, had stayed for a 
long time with a tribe in Central Africa. Upon his return he showed 
me the fine objects he had brought back with him, adding: “But I 
am still waiting to get some very excellent things. Some of the tribes- 
men with whom I was friendly have agreed to make for me and to 
send along to me a complete native hut, with all the customary 
utensils and art decorations. 
I want them for an exhibit in my 
museum. They have agreed to construct all the objects to one-third 
of their usual size, because I have not sufficient space for a full-scale 
model. I should like to show them to you when they arrive. You will 
enjoy them.” 
After several months the objects arrived. Everything was in order, 
all the things had been reduced to one-third their original size. But 
he was puzzled about an incorrectness in certain articles. The pot 
for cooking and the wooden support on which one rests one’s head 
during sleep came in their actual size. 
The anthropologist wrote a letter to a friend, a missionary who 
lived with these people, asking him to give the tribesmen a friendly 
scolding, and to have them send the few incorrect articles in the 
proper proportions. It was a long time before the answer came. It 
read: “I was not able to get the men to do it. They insisted that 
while one might still be able to live in the reduced tent, it would 
be nonsense to make a cooking pot (already quite small in its original 
size) so small. Similarly for the wooden support.” The missionary 
wrote that he had found no way of convincing them to do it. 
Another example: An anthropologist, working on the construction 
of the grammar of a native language, had the assistance of a native 
who was able to help him with the translation of stories and sen- 
tences. One time the assistant was to translate a certain sentence, 
but the anthropologist was unable to get it from him. Puzzled, he 
tried to find out what words or grammatical inflections might be 
causing the trouble. It was only after some time that the native burst 
out, “How can I translate this sentence of yours: “The white man 
shot six bears today’? This is nonsense. It is impossible that the 
white man could shoot six bears in one day!” 
In these instances the operation, the goal itself, is viewed within 
TEACHING ABOUT AREA 
275 
the functional meaning of the whole situation, without abstracting 
from the function it has. 
In short, as I formulated in an investigation about the thinking 
of primitive peoples,! there is a great difference between fulfilling 
a task if it is sensibly required in the actual life situation, or if it 
fits into it, and performing a task with no sensible relation to the 
actual situation, or which even contradicts the given situation, mak- 
ing sense only if one abstracts entirely from its role in actual reality. 
But, as I said in that paper, it would be a mistake to conclude 
that such failure to abstract is evidence of a lack of ability to think. 
If someone refuses to perform an abstraction that is to him senseless, 
if he is not able and not willing to deal with such abstractions, this 
may be a sign that his thinking takes the situation seriously. Of 
course, in our scientific history abstraction from reality has been 
developed in various ways as a very important tool. But an inability 
or a refusal to perform steps if the real meaning of the abstraction 
in the scientific context cannot be realized is not bad but good 
thinking. Lack of abstraction in itself is no measure of thinking. The 
best thinkers refuse, and sometimes are not able, to perform tasks 
that involve really senseless abstractions; they revolt against it. 
And so, some types of children need to be introduced to geometri- 
cal problems by way of actual situations in which the task itself has 
real meaning for them. 
But there are many children—and grown-ups—who do not need 
such help. They easily become interested in the “theoretical” prob- 
lems. They take the problem as an interesting task, as a challenge 
to their productive activity. And while learning geometry they are 
able, and even eager, to apply what they have gained of insight to 
other geometrical problems and to life problems. (The extreme of 
this is, of course, the foolish attempt to apply such learned procedures 
anywhere, whether they fit or not.) 
It is, I think, a task of education to develop in children such a 
“theoretical” interest of the first kind. It opens to them a marvelous 
tealm of crystal pureness, of inherent consistency. And I think that 
1M. Wertheimer, “Uber das Denken der Naturvélker, Zahlen und Zahlgebilde,” 
Zeitschrift fiir Psychologie, 1912, Vol. 60, pp. 321-378; reprinted in: M. Wert- 
heimer, Drei Abhandlungen zur Gestalttheorie, Erlangen: Philosophische Akad- 
emie, 1925. 
276 
PRODUCTIVE THINKING 
the thesis of formal education was right in holding that thinking w'th 
pure materials is of importance generally for the development of 
thinking; one is then not so easily a victim of hazy, muddled think- 
ing even in practical situations. 
1. It has happened a number of times that teachers of mathematics 
have come to me and told me how dissatisfied they were with the 
traditional ways of teaching. They then went on to say that they 
had read or heard of my theories and had the feeling these might 
help to teach more reasonably. But they were at a loss to see how 
© 
this could be done, how a concrete teaching procedure could be 
developed in the light of gestalt theory. 
In fact, I should think that the main positive consequences for 
teaching are already implied in the preceding discussions of this book. 
I shall try to sketch here one way of proceeding which meets the 
theoretical requirements that have been developed. But I may say 
at once: there are good teachers who do similar things out of genuine 
feeling for what is required educationally. Most of what I shall 
propose is in no way surprisingly “new.” But it is certainly in many 
ways different from the actual procedures in many schools. 
Many good procedures are possible, and different children require 
sometimes different (though structurally similar) ways. It is, of course, 
easiest to teach one child alone. Here I shall speak of such teaching. 
But it is also possible, and sometimes very advisable, to employ group 
thinking in the classroom. 
In the light of my teaching experiences it would seem best— 
especially at the start—to show, to “teach” as little as possible. It 
would seem desirable to avoid as much as possible the giving of 
ready-made steps. The child should be confronted with tasks with 
which he tries to deal himself. Let the child face problems, let him 
receive co-operative help as he needs it, but let him not simply copy 
or repeat given procedures. I would avoid as much as possible any- 
thing that might introduce a mechanical state of mind, an attitude 
of drill. 
I shall illustrate with the problem of finding the area of a figure. 
What is most desirable is that the child should find his way himself 
after he has been in a structurally reasonable problem situation. If 
TEACHING ABOUT AREA 
277 
the child seems at a loss or declares: “I can’t do it,” it often suffices 
simply to say: “Try it; perhaps you'll find a way.” And if this does 
not help, one can add the question: “Where is the trouble?” And 
only if such procedures fail should one go on to give concrete help. 
2. Sensible introduction of the concept “size of area” of the rec- 
tangle. I would not start by teaching how the area of the rectangle 
is to be found, especially not with the details of determining the area 
of a rectangle. For the sense of the concept “size of area” may not 
be at all clear to the child. I would rather start with a situation in 
which the problem of being “bigger” or “‘smaller” is sensibly included. 
For example, I would give two rectangles with the same base, one 
obviously higher than the other. 
aba 
And I would ask: “How can one find exactly how much bigger the 
second figure is than the first?” Without, of course, giving the auxiliary 
line. I would have the two rectangles cut out of cardboard and placed 
side by side, letting the child find the idea of placing one on top 
of the other, fitting them to each other, and noting the remainder. 
Then I would turn to real measuring. “This figure, you see, is 
9 inches wide and so is the other. Now the height of one measures 
5 inches, the other 6 inches.” And I would at first enjoy hearing 
the child say simply: “The one is bigger by one strip” or “by one 
strip which is 9 inches wide and 1 inch high.” 
2a. I may interrupt here. For many children this abstract procedure 
is, as was mentioned above, proper. For other children it is advisable 
to start instead with a problem situation in which they can feel the 
sense concretely. For example, I would start by telling a story: “Once 
upon a time” (or still better, for some children: “In the neighbor- 
hood some days ago”) “there was a farmer who wanted to move away. 
He found a farmer in another place who was willing to exchange 
land with him. The two farms were alike in many ways and were 
about the same size. In talking over the matter, they wanted to find 
Fic. 171 
278 
PRODUCTIVE THINKING 
out exactly whether one farm was bigger than the other, and by how 
much. Here is a picture of the two farms. 
Fic,. 172 
Now how could you find out which one was the bigger?” 
The child may, instead of answering, ask questions, such as: 
“Would the farmer who had the smaller farm have enough money to 
pay for the difference?” But in most cases one can easily make the 
child face the problem of comparing the sizes. 
At this point some children arrive at the idea of breaking the figure 
up into small squares. 
2b. If this does not work, another concrete situation, involving 
more concrete help, may be tried. “You are sitting on the floor with 
another boy, and each of you is building a wall with blocks. You 
have used up all your blocks, while the other still has a pile of unused 
blocks beside him. You want very much to build your wall one block 
higher, and you ask the other boy for some of his blocks. He refuses 
to give them, and you say to him: ‘I don’t want many; you have a 
good many that you aren’t using; why can’t you let me have some?’ 
The other boy snaps back: ‘How many do you need?’ Now, how 
many do you need if you want to build your wall one block higher, 
or two blocks higher?” 
Some teachers may be aghast here at the mixing up of three- 
dimensional and two dimensional matters. One could, of course, have 
started with cardboard squares; but it does not seem to matter; in 
fact, I myself would prefer to use the blocks. 
| 
Fic. 173 
TEACHING ABOUT AREA 
279 
3. Reaching the “formula.” With such tasks, and preferably, if 
possible, with purely abstract tasks, I would try to have the child 
himself reach the formulation: “I need one more row (or two more 
rows, etc.). What I need is so many rows, and the number of rows 
has to be multiplied by the number of blocks in one row.” 
Then I would ask: “How many little squares are there in this 
whole figure?” or: “What is the whole area?” The child may then 
say: “One has to measure the base; one has to measure the altitude; 
and multiply.” 
4. Here I would let the child make the discovery that it clicks 
both ways, whether one or the other side is taken as base. 
Fic. 174 
It is often nice to see how the child enjoys finding that it works both 
ways. Under certain conditions the discovery that ab=ba is a dis- 
covery, does give insight.” 
5. Tasks at issue. I would not go on to the figuring out of answers 
to many more examples of this kind, because of the danger that the 
child might lose the structural character of the formula. Instead I 
would give at the start some interestingly different cases. And I would 
introduce another example to which the procedure does not apply, 
waiting until the child decides for himself: “I can’t do it the same 
way here; you have to count out the little squares.” I would give 
tasks such as determining the size of a room, or of two tables, or even 
the finding of the cubic volume of a room, or of a three-dimensional 
pile of blocks. In the problem one would focus on the. number of 
blocks in one square which is to be multiplied by the altitude, and 
not simply on the multiplication of the three sides. 
6. The area of the parallelogram. It seems best simply to put the 
2 Cf. M. Wertheimer, op. cit. 
280 
PRODUCTIVE THINKING 
question: “What is the area of such a figure? Can you find out?” As 
in the case of the rectangle, some children will, after a little delibera- 
tion and encouragement, find the way themselves. 
If the child makes no headway, one can ask: “What is the trouble? 
What makes it so hard?” To which the child might answer: “You see, 
the trouble is with these ends here. If the ends were like those of 
the rectangle, all would be well.” 
6a. In some cases it is helpful to give the following figure: 
Ls Sa laa ie 
Fics }75 
Children sometimes respond: “Oh, everything is all right in the 
middle, but...” 
6b. Or: “There is a house of blocks, the top having the shape of a 
rectangle. I would like to put a nice roof on it. Here I have a piece 
of reddish-brown cardboard. Maybe we can use it. It has the same 
length as the top of the house, but unfortunately it has the form of 
a parallelogram. Can you make the right kind of cover out of it?” 
A possibly better procedure, because it gives less help, might be: 
“Here is this cardboard parallelogram. What can you do to make 
a rectangle out of it?” 
6c. Alternate procedure. After having simply put the problem of 
finding the area of the parallelogram, with no result, I place an 
entirely different figure before the child, in which there are two 
structural disturbances, one clearly a not-fitting addition, the other 
a hole or gap. 
Fic. 176 
The transition from such a structurally easier task to the apparently 
different case of the parallelogram is, for some children, difficult or 
impossible without further help. But there are children who, having 
TEACHING ABOUT AREA 
281 
solved these problems, look back at the parallelogram, smile—and 
do it. 
6d. If need be, I would introduce the problem in practical terms: 
“A mechanic who makes metal plates (of rectangular form) uses 
' the following procedure to determine how much metal he will need 
for a certain size of rectangle. (Here follows the teaching of the area 
of the rectangle.) One day he is asked to make a plate of the following 
form. 
reo ly7 
He would like to find out how much metal he will need in this case. 
(Or, similarly, for the question of weight, etc.) 
“At first he is at a loss. ‘How can I do this?’ he asks. But after a 
short while the mechanic grins. He has found the way. How did 
he do it?” But I may add that I would not give a problem like this 
to many children. For many the thing is too obvious. They do not 
need the long-drawn-out story, which may be an amusing under- 
estimation of their ability. 
These figures offer considerable help toward grasping the structural 
character of “disturbance,” “gap,” “there is needed here just what is 
an unnecessary addition there.” 
At this point I would also introduce a figure to which the procedure 
does not fit, leaving it entirely to the child to see the point.

Fie. 179 
7. Procedure in giving the auxiliary lines. In most of the cases that 
I have seen, such procedures did lead to the insight: change of the 
parallelogram into the rectangle. Only if all these forms of help failed 
would I give the child concretely the steps he should have found out. 
But I would not start by stating that two verticals are to be dropped. 
I would say first that in order to get a rectangle we have to correct the 
two ends. Then I would again wait to see if the child came forward 
himself with the next step. 
Or I would ask: “How could we change it into a rectangle at one 
end?” If this did not help, I would start by cutting off the left end, 
and after waiting a moment, ask: “And what of the other side? Do 
we now have a rectangle at the other side?” 
Should there still be no progress, 
I would draw all the needed 
lines, giving both of the following figures. 
ries 
/ 
/ 
i= 
Fic. 180 
If the child has not reacted to the last step, I would ask: “What 
about the size of the two figures?” Then: “What are the parts of the 
parallelogram? Of the rectangle?” 
TEACHING ABOUT AREA 
283 
8. After the solution: remarks on repetition and drill. After the 
goal of finding the area of the parallelogram has been reached, I 
would give the pupil a number of figures, differing in appearance, 
for solution. But I would not have him repeat the solution with a 
great many exercises. Rather, I would give a few problems which 
require finding the area, making them as interestingly different as 
possible, and interspersing among them some tasks which cannot 
be solved in this way. And without any formal introduction, quite 
innocently, I would intersperse the task of finding the area of the 
trapezoid and the triangle. 
Breer ses focaeto 
Fic. 181 
There are cases in which the child, without further teaching, solves 
these problems genuinely. If he does not, procedures similar to the 
above can be used. 
One might suppose that to mix such varied tasks would be too 
much for the brain of a child. Some would say, that the child should 
stick for a time to the area of the rectangle alone, then for a con- 
siderable time to the parallelogram alone. And they would regard 
the procedure of interspersing other, even “impossible,” tasks as 
psychologically dangerous, on the ground that one should stick to a 
thing and give it much repetition, before turning to a new task. 
According to my experience, this would be true only for certain 
kinds of children, for example, for those who are too timid. In such 
cases one needs to proceed more slowly. It is important not to go 
ahead before one feels that the matter has clicked for the child. 
(But repetition need not, of itself, make for clicking.) But with many 
children the desired procedure would seem to be just the opposite. 
To deal again and again with tasks which tiresomely repeat things 
the child feels he has already grasped becomes very boresome, and 
frequently encourages the child to work thoughtlessly. It is, I would 
suppose, one of the reasons why so many children, after some school- 
ing, acquire such a hearty dislike for arithmetic and geometry. Yet 
284 
PRODUCTIVE THINKING 
with the proceedings described here, children are happy about their 
activities, their discoveries. 
The Proof 
1. Basic difficulties. The transition to the geometrical proof, to 
the “demonstration,” should be made cautiously. The child may in 
all probability not catch the sense of “demonstration.” It is quite a 
problem. And even after children have reacted positively a few times, 
I doubt whether they have a real understanding of the meaning of 
proof in the sense of the geometrician. It remains for them mostly a 
funny, not quite understandable, procedure of grown-ups. The in- 
terests of an axiomatically thinking grown-up are strange to them. 
And it does not seem really possible that they could, before acquiring 
a further knowledge and more “concrete” understanding of a large 
number of various geometrical problems, realize the aims of the 
mathematician which make this procedure sensible. 
Nevertheless, there are sensible ways of helping children to under- 
stand the need for some kind of “proof,” even if the traditional 
proofs are really understood by only a few. 
2. Introduction to proof. The proof should not be simply imposed 
on the child. It might at least be introduced in terms like these: 
“Sometimes we cannot really ‘cut up’ and ‘fill in.’ How would you 
make sure in such a case that you are correct?” It might even be 
well to introduce a figure about which one is actually not sure, and 
say: “How would you make sure that the method you have used 
Paar 
Fic. 182 
before will apply here?” To this the child may answer: “If these two 
oblique lines are parallel, then it must be right to do what we did 
before.” And if the child is now asked: “Why? Why are you so 
sure of this?” he may reply: “What matters is that what I want to 
take away from the left side should fit exactly at the right side.” If 
TEACHING ABOUT AREA 
285 
you then ask: “How could you prove that? What does that imply?” 
you may get the answer: “We have to have the two triangles equal.” 
Question: “Could you prove that they are equal if the lines are 
parallel?” Answer: “Because they were made that way.” Question: 
“Could you show in detail what was decisive in making them equal?” 
And then the child may face the question of how to get at the 
congruence or, in his terms, at the identity of the triangles, by way 
of the equality of the lines and the angles. 
The child may here make use of some general theorems he Lad 
learned earlier, such as the theorem concerning the equality of angles 
between parallel lines. If not, the child may come to these problems 
in this very context. 
It is not intended to say that the child should always, in all cases, 
find the proof himself. (Though the frequent argument that this 
would be too time consuming seems to me factually not quite true, 
nor decisive.) There is nothing against having the teacher demonstrate 
the whole procedure. But if so, he should do it in a structurally 
proper way, in order to help toward a real understanding of the 
hierarchy of the steps. 
APPENDIX 5 
The Balancing Stick 
Ir you ask children to make a T out of blocks, standing one up 
vertically, and balancing a second horizontally on top of the first, it 
is interesting to watch developments in the subjects, to watch them 
come to a grasp of the structural requirement of symmetry for stability. 
The same problem is involved in the task of carrying long sticks 
(which is interesting to study in dogs, too). First the children experi- 
ment with the stick; often they give up after some negative trials. 
But some persist, and most come back to it after a time. It is en- 
lightening to see how they learn from their mistakes. Negative trials 
are not just negative instances. Of course, sometimes a child proceeds 
with blind changes, but very often we observe sensible proceedings 
in the changes. For example, the stick was seized at the left of center, 
and it falls down on the nght; in the next attempt, the child may 
blindly repeat, grasping it at the same place—or even further to the 
left—but often children correct themselves sensibly; they grasp it 
further to the right. They may not go far enough, or they may overdo 
it, but then they correct sensibly again in the next attempt. Often 
we observe a consistent procedure in the whole behavior. There is a 
fundamental difference between a succession of random trials and a 
sensible structure in the succession of trials. 
To illustrate: If somebody shoots without seeing the target, and 
without being able to see where his shot hit, beside the target, being 
told only (or experiencing somehow) that the target was not hit by 
his shot, he will be at a loss to change the direction of his next shot 
* This appendix, which was not included in the original edition, is a slightly 
edited version of a fragment found among Max Wertheimer’s papers—M. W. 
286 
THE BALANCING STICK 
287 
sensibly. But if he can observe the effects of his negative trial, then 
the directions in which he chooses to aim do show a difference be- 
tween foolish and sensible behavior. Suppose his first shot hits at 
point 1, to the nght of the target. To choose then, for the next shot 
a direction still more to the right would be foolish. 
Zz 
t 
3 
l 
Sensibly he will aim next time so as to hit at 2 or 3, correcting his aim 
on the basis of the deviation, and continuing thus to come nearer 
to the target. 
In many children, in the blocks or the long stick problem, we see 
very soon a considerable tendency to focus on about the middle. 
If a hidden factor is introduced arbitrarily, difficulties naturally 
arise. But even in experimentation with such a changed stick, sen- 
sible procedures often occur (for example if a piece of lead is hidden 
somewhere inside the stick). But succeeding in learning what to do 
in such a situation may not provide for reasonable guesses in other 
situations, in which the location of the lead may be somewhere else 
again, since the place is arbitrary. 
If, on the other hand, a clear asymmetry is provided in the visual 
appearance of the object, as for example in these drawings, the situa- 
Fic. 183 
tion is quite different. Here we do not observe, from the outset, a 
tendency to grasp just the middle, but instead, rather a trend to 
compensate somehow. 
Now it makes a difference whether some external connection is 
arbitrarily introduced, or a sensible correlation is at work. The situa- 
tion becomes theoretically ambiguous if a child has learned, for 
example, to seize the stick, in order to carry it, on a red dot which 
is in the middle; or if he has learned to grasp a stick, one half of 
which is red and the other half green, where the colors meet. But 
now we may introduce a variation and see what happens. 
U 
288 
PRODUCTIVE THINKING 
 RREREERK SETI 
ae ee) 
[eOe a eed 
LLL 
lite l ode a rine 
eS LL 
Fic. 184 
Here the color conditions are the same, only the length of one 
end is increased. Will the children grasp at the color mark? If we 
introduce two procedures of learning—according to color and ac- 
cording to symmetry in size—then the transition from the arbitrary 
to the structurally sensible seems, if one is open-minded, easier than 
the other way around. We see again that this is not just a question 
of generalization, but of how—in what direction—generalization 
works: in a structurally sensible way, or according to blind association, 
blind connection. The p-telation between symmetry and stability 
seems, basically, to be outstanding.

Fifth: The whole procedure consisted in realizing the meaning of 
the parts in the whole structure, which led to structurization of the 
whole into (a—1) parts plus the diminishing critical region. 
The result clearly holds for whatever value ss 
may have, provided 
a is greater than 1. In the series starting with 2, of course, there are 
no (a—2) parts; a—2 being zero, the series approaches 1. If S 
a 
is equal to or greater than 1, the conditions for this structurization 
THE SUM OF A SERIES 
293 
are not given—the sum will forever increase, will not approach a limit. 
But what of values between ¥2 and 1, e.g., 34? There seemed at first 
to be no way to structural realization. The way was opened by viewing 
% not as part of 1 but as 4 of 3, taking 3 as “the whole”: 
3/4 of 1 
2 
—v™_ 
1/4 of 3 
Fic. 189 
This gave the basis for understanding the fate of the critical region, 
etc., which is here in an interesting way more complex than in the 
preceding cases. But I shall not report the further steps dealing with 
this and other related problems. 
If we look back to the usual proceeding—multiplication of the series 
and subtraction—this becomes clear: Although the usual procedure 
is so very much quicker, externally so much more elegant, though it 
holds equally generally for the structures first considered and for the 
more complex instances, it is nevertheless a trick in the sense that it 
gives no direct understanding of what happens structurally. 
Of course such elegant external procedures, leading to quick solu- 
tions, are useful. But the longer way is needed in order really to under- 
stand the construction, to realize what happens. As for finding the 
solution, the first way is either a chance happening only, or a clever 
trick, the result of some valid equations, which bring the solution 
without giving insight into the nature of the series. To be sure, it may 
have started with structural insight into one feature of the series, viz., 
that it is shiftable, that if one multiplies by a or a, the same series 
appears except for the first members; but this is not sufficient to reach 
the solution by way of structural understanding.

Communication or Conflict 
CONFERENCES: THEIR NATURE, DYNAMICS, AND PLANNING 
Edited by Mary Capes 
With a Foreword by J. R. Rees 
and a Preface by Brock Chisholm 
‘In a world where more and more is done through conferences and 
where conferences are the only alternative to chaos and war, the value 
of studies like this is immeasurable. 
This book should be read at once 
by all who ever run or attend meetings, from Mr. Dag Hammarskjéld 
to the members of a parish council.’ 
Technology 
“Anyone about to organize an international conference will devour 
this book.’ 
The Manager 
30s net 
Experiences in Groups 
and Other Papers 
W. R. BION 
A collection in volume form of Dr. Bion’s classic studies in group 
behaviour. 
Dr. Bion’s formulations of theory on group behaviour and 
group goals here set out have formed the basis of much of the develop- 
ment in work with therapeutic and other kinds of group undertaken 
since the war.

The Psycholog Ly of Affiliation 
EXPERIMENTAL STUDIES OF THE SOURCES OF GREGARIOL = 
STANLEY SCHACHTER 
Research and theory concerned with conditions affecting man’ 
to associate with his fellows, the effect of group membership 
emotions, and the social determinants of emotional states.
